<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Endocrine disruptor</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NonylphenolEstradiol.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="1664" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/NonylphenolEstradiol.png/250px-NonylphenolEstradiol.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/NonylphenolEstradiol.png/375px-NonylphenolEstradiol.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/NonylphenolEstradiol.png/500px-NonylphenolEstradiol.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NonylphenolEstradiol.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A comparison of the structures of the natural hormone <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">estradiol</a> (left) and one of the <a href="/wiki/Nonylphenol" title="Nonylphenol">nonyl-phenols</a> (right), an endocrine disruptor</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Endocrine disruptors</b> are chemicals that, at certain doses, can interfere with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocrine" title="Endocrine">endocrine</a> (or hormone) systems. These disruptions can cause cancerous tumors, birth defects, and other developmental disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Any system in the body controlled by hormones can be derailed by hormone disruptors. Specifically, endocrine disruptors may be associated with the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Learning_disabilities" title="Learning disabilities">learning disabilities</a>, severe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_disorder" title="Attention deficit disorder">attention deficit disorder</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> and brain development problems; deformations of the body (including limbs); breast cancer, prostate cancer, thyroid and other cancers; sexual development problems such as <a href="/wiki/Feminization_(biology)" title="Feminization (biology)">feminizing</a> of males or <a href="/wiki/Virilization" title="Virilization">masculinizing</a> effects on females, etc.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Recently The Endocrine Society released a statement on Endocrine-Disrupting Chemicals (EDCs) specifically listing obesity, diabetes, female reproduction, male reproduction, hormone-sensitive cancers in females, prostate cancer in males, thyroid, and neurodevelopment and neuroendocrine systems as being affected biological aspects of being exposed to EDCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The critical period of development for most organisms is between the transition from a fertilized egg into a fully formed infant. As the cells begin to grow and differentiate, there are critical balances of hormones and protein changes that must occur. Therefore, a dose of disrupting chemicals may do substantial damage to a developing <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a>. The same dose may not significantly affect adult mothers.</p>
<p>There has been controversy over endocrine disruptors, with some groups calling for swift action by regulators to remove them from the market, and regulators and other scientists calling for further study. Some endocrine disruptors have been identified and removed from the market (for example, a drug called <a href="/wiki/Diethylstilbestrol" title="Diethylstilbestrol">diethylstilbestrol</a>), but it is uncertain whether some endocrine disruptors on the market actually harm humans and wildlife at the doses to which wildlife and humans are exposed. Additionally, a key scientific paper, published in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, which helped launch the movement of those opposed to endocrine disruptors, was retracted and its author found to have committed scientific misconduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12449946_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12449946-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Found in many household and industrial products, endocrine disruptors are substances that "interfere with the synthesis, secretion, transport, binding, action, or elimination of natural <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> in the body that are responsible for development, behavior, fertility, and maintenance of homeostasis (normal cell metabolism)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9539004_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9539004-4">[4]</a></sup> They are sometimes also referred to as <b>hormonally active agents</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11795392_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11795392-5">[5]</a></sup> <b>endocrine disrupting chemicals</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19502515_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19502515-6">[6]</a></sup> or <b>endocrine disrupting compounds</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlEndocrine_Disrupting_Compounds_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlEndocrine_Disrupting_Compounds-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies in cells and laboratory animals have shown that EDCs can cause adverse <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">biological</a> effects in animals, and low-level exposures may also cause similar effects in human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-8">[8]</a></sup> The term <i>endocrine disruptor</i> is often used as synonym for <a href="/wiki/Xenohormone" title="Xenohormone">xenohormone</a> although the latter can mean any naturally occurring or artificially produced compound showing hormone-like properties (usually binding to certain hormonal receptors). EDCs in the environment may also be related to reproductive and infertility problems in wildlife and bans and restrictions on their use has been associated with a reduction in health problems and the recovery of some wildlife populations.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term <i>endocrine disruptor</i> was coined at the Wingspread Conference Centre in Wisconsin, in 1991. One of the early papers on the phenomenon was by <a href="/wiki/Theo_Colborn" title="Theo Colborn">Theo Colborn</a> in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8080506_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8080506-9">[9]</a></sup> In this paper, she stated that environmental chemicals disrupt the development of the endocrine system, and that effects of exposure during development are often permanent. Although the endocrine disruption has been disputed by some,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady2010_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grady2010-10">[10]</a></sup> work sessions from 1992 to 1999 have generated consensus statements from scientists regarding the hazard from endocrine disruptors, particularly in wildlife and also in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-911131-35-3_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-911131-35-3-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17539108_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17539108-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-880611-19-8_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-880611-19-8-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9460166_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9460166-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brook_1999_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brook_1999-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Endocrine_Society" title="The Endocrine Society">The Endocrine Society</a> released a scientific statement outlining mechanisms and effects of endocrine disruptors on “male and female reproduction, breast development and cancer, prostate cancer, neuroendocrinology, thyroid, metabolism and obesity, and cardiovascular endocrinology,” and showing how experimental and epidemiological studies converge with human clinical observations “to implicate EDCs as a significant concern to public health.” The statement noted that it is difficult to show that endocrine disruptors cause human diseases, and it recommended that the <a href="/wiki/Precautionary_principle" title="Precautionary principle">precautionary principle</a> should be followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-16">[16]</a></sup> A concurrent statement expresses policy concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Endocrine disrupting compounds encompass a variety of chemical classes, including drugs, pesticides, compounds used in the <a href="/wiki/Plastics_industry" title="Plastics industry">plastics industry</a> and in consumer products, industrial by-products and pollutants, and even some naturally produced botanical chemicals. Some are pervasive and widely dispersed in the environment and may bio-accumulate. Some are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutants" title="Persistent organic pollutants">persistent organic pollutants</a> (POP's), and can be transported long distances across national boundaries and have been found in virtually all regions of the world, and may even concentrate near the North Pole, due to weather patterns and cold conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cold.2C_Clear.2C_and_Deadly_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cold.2C_Clear.2C_and_Deadly-18">[18]</a></sup> Others are rapidly degraded in the environment or human body or may be present for only short periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlREPIDISCA-Global_assessment_of_the_state-of-the-science_of_endocrine_disruptors_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlREPIDISCA-Global_assessment_of_the_state-of-the-science_of_endocrine_disruptors-19">[19]</a></sup> Health effects attributed to endocrine disrupting compounds include a range of reproductive problems (reduced fertility, male and female <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_tract" title="Reproductive tract">reproductive tract</a> abnormalities, and skewed male/female <a href="/wiki/Sex_ratio" title="Sex ratio">sex ratios</a>, loss of fetus, menstrual problems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.cranfield.ac.uk_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.cranfield.ac.uk-20">[20]</a></sup>); changes in hormone levels; early puberty; brain and behavior problems; impaired immune functions; and various cancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urle.hormone_.7C_EDC_Human_Effects_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-urle.hormone_.7C_EDC_Human_Effects-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>One example of the consequences of the exposure of developing animals, including humans, to hormonally active agents is the case of the drug <a href="/wiki/Diethylstilbestrol" title="Diethylstilbestrol">diethylstilbestrol</a> (DES), a non-steroidal estrogen and not an environmental pollutant. Prior to its ban in the early 1970s, doctors prescribed DES to as many as five million pregnant women to block spontaneous abortion, an <a href="/wiki/Off-label_use" title="Off-label use">off-label use</a> of this medication prior to 1947. It was discovered after the children went through puberty that DES affected the development of the reproductive system and caused <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_cancer" title="Vaginal cancer">vaginal cancer</a>. The relevance of the DES saga to the risks of exposure to endocrine disruptors is questionable, as the doses involved are much higher in these individuals than in those due to environmental exposures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9557209_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9557209-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aquatic" title="wikt:aquatic">Aquatic</a> life subjected to endocrine disruptors in an urban effluent have experienced decreased levels of <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> and increased feminization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WHO" title="WHO">WHO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> released a study, the most comprehensive report on EDCs to date, calling for more research to fully understand the associations between EDCs and the risks to health of human and animal life. The team pointed to wide gaps in knowledge and called for more research to obtain a fuller picture of the health and environmental impacts of endocrine disruptors. To improve global knowledge the team has recommended:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Testing: known EDCs are only the 'tip of the iceberg' and more comprehensive testing methods are required to identify other possible endocrine disruptors, their sources, and routes of exposure.</i></li>
<li><i>Research: more scientific evidence is needed to identify the effects of mixtures of EDCs on humans and wildlife (mainly from industrial by-products) to which humans and wildlife are increasingly exposed.</i></li>
<li><i>Reporting: many sources of EDCs are not known because of insufficient reporting and information on chemicals in products, materials and goods.</i></li>
<li><i>Collaboration: more data sharing between scientists and between countries can fill gaps in data, primarily in developing countries and emerging economies.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Endocrine_system">Endocrine system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Endocrine system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">Endocrine system</a></div>
<p>Endocrine systems are found in most varieties of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>. The endocrine system consists of <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">glands</a> that secrete <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptors</a> that detect and react to the hormones.</p>
<p>Hormones travel throughout the body and act as chemical messengers. Hormones interface with cells that contain matching receptors in or on their surfaces. The hormone binds with the receptor, much like a key would fit into a lock. The endocrine system regulates adjustments through slower internal processes, using hormones as messengers. The endocrine system secretes hormones in response to environmental stimuli and to orchestrate developmental and reproductive changes. The adjustments brought on by the endocrine system are biochemical, changing the cell's internal and external chemistry to bring about a long term change in the body. These systems work together to maintain the proper functioning of the body through its entire life cycle. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_steroids" title="Sex steroids">Sex steroids</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrogens" title="Estrogens">estrogens</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Androgens" title="Androgens">androgens</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> hormones, are subject to <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">feedback</a> regulation, which tends to limit the sensitivity of these glands.</p>
<p>Hormones work at very small doses (part per billion ranges). Endocrine disruption can thereby also occur from low-dose exposure to exogenous hormones or hormonally active chemicals that can interfere with receptors for other hormonally mediated processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Furthermore, since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenous" title="Endogenous">endogenous</a> hormones are already present in the body in biologically active concentrations, additional exposure to relatively small amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exogenous" title="Exogenous">exogenous</a> hormonally active substances can disrupt the proper functioning of the body's endocrine system. Thus, an endocrine disruptor can elicit adverse effects at much lower doses than a toxicity, acting through a different mechanism.</p>
<p>The timing of exposure is also critical. Most critical stages of development occur in utero, where the fertilized egg divides, rapidly developing every structure of a fully formed baby, including much of the wiring in the brain. Interfering with the hormonal communication in utero can have profound effects both structurally and toward brain development. Depending on the stage of reproductive development, interference with hormonal signaling can result in irreversible effects not seen in adults exposed to the same dose for the same length of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guo_1995_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guo_1995-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigsby_1999_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bigsby_1999-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18848954_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18848954-28">[28]</a></sup> Experiments with animals have identified critical developmental time points in utero and days after birth when exposure to chemicals that interfere with or mimic hormones have adverse effects that persist into adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigsby_1999_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bigsby_1999-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eriksson_1991_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eriksson_1991-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Recabarren_2008_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Recabarren_2008-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szabo_2009_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szabo_2009-31">[31]</a></sup> Disruption of thyroid function early in development may be the cause of abnormal sexual development in both males<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lilienthal_2006_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lilienthal_2006-32">[32]</a></sup> and females<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talsness_2005_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talsness_2005-33">[33]</a></sup> early motor development impairment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eriksson_2002_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eriksson_2002-34">[34]</a></sup> and learning disabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16611620_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16611620-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are studies of cell cultures, laboratory animals, wildlife, and accidentally exposed humans that show that environmental chemicals cause a wide range of reproductive, developmental, growth, and behavior effects, and so while "endocrine disruption in humans by pollutant chemicals remains largely undemonstrated, the underlying science is sound and the potential for such effects is real."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12837917_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12837917-36">[36]</a></sup> While compounds that produce estrogenic, androgenic, antiandrogenic, and antithyroid actions have been studied, less is known about interactions with other hormones.</p>
<p>The interrelationship between exposures to chemicals and health effects are rather complex. It is hard to definitively link a particular chemical with a specific health effect, and exposed adults may not show any ill effects. But, fetuses and embryos, whose growth and development are highly controlled by the endocrine system, are more vulnerable to exposure and may suffer overt or subtle lifelong health and/or reproductive abnormalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1425407_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1425407-37">[37]</a></sup> Prebirth exposure, in some cases, can lead to permanent alterations and adult diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colborn_2007_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colborn_2007-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some in the scientific community are concerned that exposure to endocrine disruptors in the womb or early in life may be associated with neurodevelopmental disorders including reduced IQ, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ADHD" title="ADHD">ADHD</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.iceh.org_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.iceh.org-39">[39]</a></sup> Certain cancers and uterine abnormalities in women are associated with exposure to <a href="/wiki/Diethylstilbestrol" title="Diethylstilbestrol">Diethylstilbestrol</a> (DES) in the womb due to DES used as a medical treatment.</p>
<p>In another case, <a href="/wiki/Phthalate" title="Phthalate">phthalates</a> in pregnant women’s urine was linked to subtle, but specific, genital changes in their male infants – a shorter, more female-like <a href="/wiki/Anogenital_distance" title="Anogenital distance">anogenital distance</a> and associated incomplete descent of testes and a smaller scrotum and penis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swan_2005_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swan_2005-40">[40]</a></sup> The science behind this study has been questioned by phthalate industry consultants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16393642_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16393642-41">[41]</a></sup> As of June 2008, there are only five studies of anogenital distance in humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18519505_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18519505-42">[42]</a></sup> and one researcher has stated "Whether AGD measures in humans relate to clinically important outcomes, however, remains to be determined, as does its utility as a measure of androgen action in epidemiologic studies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17439530_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17439530-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="U-shaped_dose-response_curve">U-shaped dose-response curve</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: U-shaped dose-response curve">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most toxicants, including endocrine disruptors, have been claimed to follow a U-shaped <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dose-response_relationship" title="Dose-response relationship">dose response curve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This means that very low and very high levels have more effects than mid-level exposure to a toxicant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Endocrine disrupting effects have been noted in animals exposed to environmentally relevant levels of some chemicals. For example, a common <a href="/wiki/Flame_retardant" title="Flame retardant">flame retardant</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PBDE" title="PBDE">BDE</a>-47, affects the reproductive system and thyroid gland of female rats in doses of the order of those to which humans are exposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18335096_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18335096-46">[46]</a></sup> Low concentrations of endocrine disruptors can also have synergistic effects in amphibians, but it is not clear that this is an effect mediated through the endocrine system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16818245_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16818245-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics have argued that data suggest that the amounts of chemicals in the environment are too low to cause an effect. A consensus statement by the Learning and Developmental Disabilities Initiative argued that "The very low-dose effects of endocrine disruptors cannot be predicted from high-dose studies, which contradicts the standard 'dose makes the poison' rule of toxicology. Nontraditional dose-response curves are referred to as nonmonotonic dose response curves."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.iceh.org_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.iceh.org-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The dosage objection could also be overcome if low concentrations of different endocrine disruptors are synergistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid8633243_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid8633243-48">[48]</a></sup> This paper was published in <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a> in June 1996, and was one reason for the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Food_Quality_Protection_Act" title="Food Quality Protection Act">Food Quality Protection Act</a> of 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlW._Alton_Jones_Foundation_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlW._Alton_Jones_Foundation-49">[49]</a></sup> The results could not be confirmed with the same and alternative methodologies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9075711_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9075711-50">[50]</a></sup> and the original paper was retracted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9254413_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9254413-51">[51]</a></sup> with Arnold found to have committed scientific misconduct by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Office_of_Research_Integrity" title="United States Office of Research Integrity">United States Office of Research Integrity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12449946_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12449946-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been claimed that <a href="/wiki/Tamoxifen" title="Tamoxifen">Tamoxifen</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phthalates" title="Phthalates">phthalates</a> have fundamentally different (and harmful) effects on the body at low doses than at high doses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Routes_of_exposure">Routes of exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Routes of exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Food is a major mechanism by which people are exposed to pollutants. Diet is thought to account for up to 90% of a person's <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">PCB</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> body burden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F.C3.BCrst_2006_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-F.C3.BCrst_2006-53">[53]</a></sup> In a study of 32 different common food products from three grocery stores in Dallas, fish and other animal products were found to be contaminated with <a href="/wiki/Polybrominated_diphenyl_ethers" title="Polybrominated diphenyl ethers">PBDE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schecter_2004_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schecter_2004-54">[54]</a></sup> Since these compounds are fat soluble, it is likely they are accumulating from the environment in the fatty tissue of animals we eat. Some suspect fish consumption is a major source of many environmental contaminates. Indeed, both wild and farmed salmon from all over the world have been shown to contain a variety of man-made organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14716013_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14716013-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the increase in household products containing pollutants and the decrease in the quality of building ventilation, indoor air has become a significant source of pollutant exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weschler2009_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weschler2009-56">[56]</a></sup> Residents living in homes with wood floors treated in the 1960s with PCB-based wood finish have a much higher body burden than the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudel_2008_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudel_2008-57">[57]</a></sup> A study of indoor house dust and dryer lint of 16 homes found high levels of all 22 different PBDE congeners tested for in all samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15773463_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15773463-58">[58]</a></sup> Recent studies suggest that contaminated house dust, not food, may be the major source of PBDE in our bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2008_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2008-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morland_2005_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morland_2005-60">[60]</a></sup> One study estimated that ingestion of house dust accounts for up to 82% of our PBDE body burden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lorber_2008_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lorber_2008-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research conducted by the Environmental Working Group found that 19 out of 20 children tested had levels of PBDE in their blood 3.5 times higher than the amount in their mothers' blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlFire_Retardants_in_Toddlers_and_Their_Mothers_.7C_Environmental_Working_Group_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlFire_Retardants_in_Toddlers_and_Their_Mothers_.7C_Environmental_Working_Group-62">[62]</a></sup> It has been shown that contaminated house dust is a primary source of lead in young children's bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7354967_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7354967-63">[63]</a></sup> It may be that babies and toddlers ingest more contaminated house dust than the adults they live with, and therefore have much higher levels of pollutants in their systems.</p>
<p>Consumer goods are another potential source of exposure to endocrine disruptors. An analysis of the composition of 42 household cleaning and personal care products versus 43 "chemical free" products has been performed. The products contained 55 different chemical compounds: 50 were found in the 42 conventional samples representing 170 product types, while 41 were detected in 43 "chemical free" samples representing 39 product types. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parabens" title="Parabens">Parabens</a>, a class of chemicals that has been associated with reproductive-tract issues, were detected in seven of the "chemical free" products, including three sunscreens that did not list parabens on the label. Vinyl products such as shower curtains were found to contain more than 10% by weight of the compound <a href="/wiki/Bis(2-ethylhexyl)_phthalate" title="Bis(2-ethylhexyl) phthalate">DEHP</a>, which when present in dust has been associated with asthma and wheezing in children. The risk of exposure to EDCs increases as products, both conventional and "chemical free," are used in combination. "If a consumer used the alternative surface cleaner, tub and tile cleaner, laundry detergent, bar soap, shampoo and conditioner, facial cleanser and lotion, and toothpaste [he or she] would potentially be exposed to at least 19 compounds: 2 parabens, 3 <a href="/wiki/Phthalate" title="Phthalate">phthalates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cysteamine" title="Cysteamine">MEA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanolamine" title="Ethanolamine">DEA</a>, 5 <a href="/wiki/Alkylphenol" title="Alkylphenol">alkylphenols</a>, and 7 fragrances."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22398195_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22398195-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>An analysis of the endocrine disrupting chemicals in <a href="/wiki/Old_Order_Mennonite" title="Old Order Mennonite">Old Order Mennonite</a> women in mid-pregnancy determined that they have much lower levels in their systems than the general population. Mennonites eat mostly fresh, unprocessed foods, farm without pesticides, and use few or no cosmetics or personal care products. One woman who had reported using hairspray and perfume had high levels of monoethyl phthalate, while the other women all had levels below detection. Three women who reported being in a car or truck within 48 hours of providing a urine sample had higher levels of diethylhexyl phthalate which is found in polyvinyl chloride, and is used in car interiors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22739065_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22739065-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additives added to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastics" title="Plastics">plastics</a> during manufacturing may leach into the environment after the plastic item is discarded; additives in <a href="/wiki/Microplastics" title="Microplastics">microplastics</a> in the ocean leach into ocean water and in plastics in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfills" title="Landfills">landfills</a> may escape and leach into the soil and then into <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19528054_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19528054-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All people are exposed to chemicals with estrogenic effects in their everyday life, because endocrine disrupting chemicals are found in low doses in thousands of products. Chemicals commonly detected in people include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyls</a> (PCB's), <a href="/wiki/Bisphenol_A" title="Bisphenol A">bisphenol A</a> (BPA), <a href="/wiki/Polybrominated_diphenyl_ethers" title="Polybrominated diphenyl ethers">polybrominated diphenyl ethers</a> (PBDE's), and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Phthalate" title="Phthalate">phthalates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCDC_-_National_Report_on_Human_Exposure_to_Environmental_Chemicals_.7C_CDC_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCDC_-_National_Report_on_Human_Exposure_to_Environmental_Chemicals_.7C_CDC-67">[67]</a></sup> In fact, almost all plastic products, including those advertised as "BPA free", have been found to leach endocrine-disrupting chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In a 2011, study it was found that some "BPA-free" products released more endocrine active chemicals than the BPA-containing products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is some dispute in the scientific community surrounding the claim that these chemicals actually disrupt the endocrine system. Many believe that there is little evidence that the degree of exposure in humans is enough to warrant concern,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12837917_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12837917-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10856020_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10856020-71">[71]</a></sup> while many others believe there is evidence that these chemicals pose some risk to human health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11192656_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11192656-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14976101_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14976101-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15198913_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15198913-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other forms of endocrine disruptors are phytoestrogens (plant hormones).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some researchers are investigating the health risks to children of endocrine disrupting chemicals. Bisphenol A, until 2010 a common component in the plastic used to manufacture plastic <a href="/wiki/Baby_bottle" title="Baby bottle">baby bottles</a>, has been banned in most countries. In 2010, despite strong industry opposition, Canada was the first to ban BPA's use in baby bottles. Australia and the European Union followed in 2011. Several states in the United States had banned its use by 2011, and in 2012 a nationwide ban was put in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlnews.findlaw.com_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlnews.findlaw.com-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Xenoestrogens">Xenoestrogens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Xenoestrogens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Xenoestrogen" title="Xenoestrogen">Xenoestrogen</a></div>
<p><b>Xenoestrogens</b> are a type of <a href="/wiki/Xenohormone" title="Xenohormone">xenohormone</a> that imitates <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a>. Synthetic xenoestrogens include widely used industrial compounds, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyls" title="Polychlorinated biphenyls">PCBs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bisphenol_A" title="Bisphenol A">BPA</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phthalates" title="Phthalates">phthalates</a>, which have estrogenic effects on a living organism.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alkylphenols">Alkylphenols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Alkylphenols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkylphenols" title="Alkylphenols">Alkylphenols</a></div>
<p>Alkylphenols are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenoestrogens" title="Xenoestrogens">xenoestrogens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The European Union has implemented sales and use restrictions on certain applications in which <a href="/wiki/Nonylphenol" title="Nonylphenol">nonylphenols</a> are used because of their alleged "toxicity, persistence, and the liability to bioaccumulate" but the United States EPA has taken a slower approach to make sure that action is based on "sound science".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACSdebate_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-ACSdebate-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The long-chain alkylphenols are used extensively as precursors to the <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a>, as additives for fuels and <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricants</a>, polymers, and as components in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenolic_resin" title="Phenolic resin">phenolic resins</a>. These compounds are also used as building block chemicals that are also used in making <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fragrances" title="Fragrances">fragrances</a>, thermoplastic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastomers" title="Elastomers">elastomers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidants</a>, oil field chemicals and <a href="/wiki/Fire_retardant" title="Fire retardant">fire retardant</a> materials. Through the downstream use in making alkylphenolic resins, alkylphenols are also found in tires, adhesives, coatings, carbonless copy paper and high performance rubber products. They have been used in industry for over 40 years.</p>
<p>Certain alkylphenols are degradation products from nonionic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detergents" title="Detergents">detergents</a>. Nonylphenol is considered to be a low-level endocrine disruptor owing to its tendency to mimic estrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18282600_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18282600-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.pca.state.mn.us_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.pca.state.mn.us-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bisphenol_A_.28BPA.29">Bisphenol A (BPA)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bisphenol A (BPA)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
