<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg"><img alt="Rod of Asclepius2.svg" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="251" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg/12px-Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg/18px-Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg/25px-Rod_of_Asclepius2.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bisphenol_A" title="Bisphenol A">Bisphenol A</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bisphenol_A.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="1457" height="72" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bisphenol_A.svg/220px-Bisphenol_A.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bisphenol_A.svg/330px-Bisphenol_A.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bisphenol_A.svg/440px-Bisphenol_A.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bisphenol_A.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bisphenol A chemical structure</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bisphenol A is commonly found in plastic bottles, plastic food containers, dental materials, and the linings of metal <a href="/wiki/Tin_can" title="Tin can">food and infant formula cans</a>. Another exposure comes from receipt paper commonly used at grocery stores and restaurants, because today the paper is commonly coated with a BPA containing clay for printing purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>BPA is a known endocrine disruptor, and numerous studies have found that laboratory animals exposed to low levels of it have elevated rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">mammary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancers</a>, decreased sperm count, reproductive problems, early <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, and neurological problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn1-58829-830-2_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn1-58829-830-2-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.E2.80.99Connor_Chapin_2003_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-O.E2.80.99Connor_Chapin_2003-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18197296_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18197296-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMAVS_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAMAVS-86">[86]</a></sup> Early developmental stages appear to be the period of greatest sensitivity to its effects, and some studies have linked prenatal exposure to later physical and neurological difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HealthCanada_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-HealthCanada-87">[87]</a></sup> Regulatory bodies have determined safety levels for humans, but those safety levels are currently being questioned or are under review as a result of new scientific studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHP_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-EHP-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19931376_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19931376-89">[89]</a></sup> A 2011 study that investigated the number of chemicals pregnant women are exposed to in the U.S. found BPA in 96% of women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010 the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> expert panel recommended no new regulations limiting or banning the use of bisphenol A, stating that "initiation of public health measures would be premature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In August 2008, the U.S. FDA issued a draft reassessment, reconfirming their initial opinion that, based on scientific evidence, it is safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlChemical_Used_in_Plastic_Bottles_Is_Safe.2C_F.D.A._Says_-_NYTimes.com_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlChemical_Used_in_Plastic_Bottles_Is_Safe.2C_F.D.A._Says_-_NYTimes.com-92">[92]</a></sup> However, in October 2008, FDA's advisory Science Board concluded that the Agency's assessment was "flawed" and hadn't proven the chemical to be safe for formula-fed infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlAdvisers:_FDA_decision_on_safety_of_BPA_flawed_-_USATODAY.com_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlAdvisers:_FDA_decision_on_safety_of_BPA_flawed_-_USATODAY.com-93">[93]</a></sup> In January 2010, the FDA issued a report indicating that, due to findings of recent studies that used novel approaches in testing for subtle effects, both the National Toxicology Program at the National Institutes of Health as well as the FDA have some level of concern regarding the possible effects of BPA on the brain and behavior of fetuses, infants and younger children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlBisphenol_A_.28BPA.29:_Use_in_Food_Contact_Application_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlBisphenol_A_.28BPA.29:_Use_in_Food_Contact_Application-94">[94]</a></sup> In 2012 the FDA did ban the use of BPA in baby bottles, however the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Working_Group" title="Environmental Working Group">Environmental Working Group</a> called the ban "purely cosmetic". In a statement they said, “If the agency truly wants to prevent people from being exposed to this toxic chemical associated with a variety of serious and chronic conditions it should ban its use in cans of infant formula, food and beverages." The <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Defense_Council" title="Natural Resources Defense Council">Natural Resources Defense Council</a> called the move inadequate saying, the FDA needs to ban BPA from all food packaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-commondreams_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-commondreams-95">[95]</a></sup> In a statement a FDA spokesman said the agency's action was not based on safety concerns and that "the agency continues to support the safety of BPA for use in products that hold food."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpo_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffpo-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bisphenol_S_.28BPS.29">Bisphenol S (BPS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bisphenol S (BPS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bisphenol_S" title="Bisphenol S">Bisphenol S</a> is an analog of bisphenol A. It is commonly found in thermal receipts, plastics, and household dust. Traces of BPS have also been found in personal care products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> It is more presently being used because of the ban of BPA. BPS is used in place of BPA in “BPA free” items. However BPS has been shown to be as much of an endocrine disruptor as BPA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DDT">DDT</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: DDT">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DDT.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="1052" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/DDT.svg/220px-DDT.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/DDT.svg/330px-DDT.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/DDT.svg/440px-DDT.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DDT.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
DDT Chemical structure</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dichlorodiphenyltrichloroethane (DDT) was first used as a pesticide against <a href="/wiki/Colorado_potato_beetle" title="Colorado potato beetle">Colorado potato beetles</a> on crops beginning in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_1971_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_1971-99">[99]</a></sup> An increase in the incidence of <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, epidemic <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> led to its use against the mosquitoes, lice, and houseflies that carried these diseases. Before World War II, <a href="/wiki/Pyrethrum" title="Pyrethrum">pyrethrum</a>, an extract of a flower from Japan, had been used to control these insects and the diseases they can spread. During World War II, Japan stopped exporting pyrethrum, forcing the search for an alternative. Fearing an epidemic outbreak of typhus, every British and American soldier was issued DDT, who used it to routinely dust beds, tents, and barracks all over the world.</p>
<p>DDT was approved for general, non-military use after the war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_1971_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis_1971-99">[99]</a></sup> It became used worldwide to increase <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> crop yields that were threatened by pest infestation, and to reduce the spread of malaria which had a high mortality rate in many parts of the world. Its use for agricultural purposes has since been prohibited by national legislation of most countries, while its use as a control against malaria vectors is permitted, as specifically stated by the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Convention_on_Persistent_Organic_Pollutants" title="Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants">Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>As early as 1946, the harmful effects of DDT on bird, beneficial insects, fish, and marine invertebrates were seen in the environment. The most infamous example of these effects were seen in the eggshells of large predatory birds, which did not develop to be thick enough to support the adult bird sitting on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lundholm_1997_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lundholm_1997-101">[101]</a></sup> Further studies found DDT in high concentrations in carnivores all over the world, the result of <a href="/wiki/Biomagnification" title="Biomagnification">biomagnification</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Szlinder-Richert_2008_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Szlinder-Richert_2008-102">[102]</a></sup> Twenty years after its widespread use, DDT was found trapped in ice samples taken from Antarctic snow, suggesting wind and water are another means of environmental transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterle_1969_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterle_1969-103">[103]</a></sup> Recent studies show the historical record of DDT deposition on remote glaciers in the Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daly_2005_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daly_2005-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>More than sixty years ago when biologists began to study the effects of DDT on laboratory animals, it was discovered that DDT interfered with reproductive development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14808278_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14808278-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoner_1953_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stoner_1953-106">[106]</a></sup> Recent studies suggest DDT may inhibit the proper development of female reproductive organs that adversely affects reproduction into maturity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiemann_2008_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tiemann_2008-107">[107]</a></sup> Additional studies suggest that a marked decrease in fertility in adult males may be due to DDT exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hallegue_2003_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hallegue_2003-108">[108]</a></sup> Most recently, it has been suggested that exposure to DDT in utero can increase a child's risk of <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">childhood obesity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verhulst_2009_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verhulst_2009-109">[109]</a></sup> DDT is still used as anti-malarial insecticide in Africa and parts of Southeast Asia in limited quantities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polychlorinated_biphenyls">Polychlorinated biphenyls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Polychlorinated biphenyls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyls" title="Polychlorinated biphenyls">Polychlorinated biphenyls</a></div>
<p>Polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs) are a class of chlorinated compounds used as industrial coolants and lubricants. PCBs are created by heating benzene, a byproduct of gasoline refining, with chlorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlMarch.2FApril_2001_Sierra_Magazine_-_Sierra_Club_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlMarch.2FApril_2001_Sierra_Magazine_-_Sierra_Club-110">[110]</a></sup> They were first manufactured commercially by the Swann Chemical Company in 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlFox_River_History_of_PCBs_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlFox_River_History_of_PCBs-111">[111]</a></sup> In 1933, the health effects of direct PCB exposure was seen in those who worked with the chemicals at the manufacturing facility in Alabama. In 1935, Monsanto acquired the company, taking over US production and licensing PCB manufacturing technology internationally.</p>
<p>General Electric was one of the largest US companies to incorporate PCBs into manufactured equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlFox_River_History_of_PCBs_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-urlFox_River_History_of_PCBs-111">[111]</a></sup> Between 1952 and 1977, the New York GE plant had dumped more than 500,000 pounds of PCB waste into the Hudson River. PCBs were first discovered in the environment far from its industrial use by scientists in Sweden studying DDT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid5388040_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid5388040-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The effects of acute exposure to PCBs were well known within the companies who used Monsanto's PCB formulation who saw the effects on their workers who came into contact with it regularly. Direct skin contact results in a severe acne-like condition called chloracne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18329192_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18329192-113">[113]</a></sup> Exposure increases the risk of skin cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loomis_1997_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loomis_1997-114">[114]</a></sup> liver cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_1987_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_1987-115">[115]</a></sup> and brain cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loomis_1997_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Loomis_1997-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinks_1992_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sinks_1992-116">[116]</a></sup> Monsanto tried for years to downplay the health problems related to PCB exposure in order to continue sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlMonsanto_Hid_Decades_Of_Pollution_.28washingtonpost.com.29_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlMonsanto_Hid_Decades_Of_Pollution_.28washingtonpost.com.29-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>The detrimental health effects of PCB exposure to humans became undeniable when two separate <a href="/wiki/Yush%C5%8D_disease" title="Yushō disease">incidents of contaminated cooking oil poisoned thousands of residents in Japan</a> (Yushō disease, 1968) and Taiwan (Yu-cheng disease, 1979),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHC002_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-EHC002-118">[118]</a></sup> leading to a worldwide ban on PCB use in 1977. Recent studies show the endocrine interference of certain PCB congeners is toxic to the liver and thyroid,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kodavanti_2006_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kodavanti_2006-119">[119]</a></sup> increases childhood obesity in children exposed prenatally,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verhulst_2009_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Verhulst_2009-109">[109]</a></sup> and may increase the risk of developing diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uemura_2008_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uemura_2008-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mullerova_2008_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mullerova_2008-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>PCBs in the environment may also be related to reproductive and infertility problems in wildlife. In Alaska it is thought that they may contribute to reproductive defects, infertility and antler malformation in some deer populations. Declines in the populations of otters and sea lions may also be partially due to their exposure to PCBs, the insecticide DDT, other persistent organic pollutants. Bans and restrictions on the use of EDCs have been associated with a reduction in health problems and the recovery of some wildlife populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polybrominated_diphenyl_ethers">Polybrominated diphenyl ethers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Polybrominated diphenyl ethers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polybrominated_diphenyl_ethers" title="Polybrominated diphenyl ethers">Polybrominated diphenyl ethers</a></div>
<p>Polybrominated diphenyl ethers (PBDEs) are a class of compounds found in <a href="/wiki/Flame_retardant" title="Flame retardant">flame retardants</a> used in plastic cases of televisions and computers, electronics, carpets, lighting, bedding, clothing, car components, foam cushions and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textiles" title="Textiles">textiles</a>. Potential health concern: PBDE's are structurally very similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyls" title="Polychlorinated biphenyls">Polychlorinated biphenyls</a> (PCBs), and have similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurotoxic" title="Neurotoxic">neurotoxic</a> effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eriksson_2006_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eriksson_2006-123">[123]</a></sup> Research has correlated halogenated hydrocarbons, such as PCBs, with neurotoxicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kodavanti_2006_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kodavanti_2006-119">[119]</a></sup> PBDEs are similar in chemical structure to PCBs, and it has been suggested that PBDEs act by the same mechanism as PCBs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kodavanti_2006_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kodavanti_2006-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1930s and 1940s, the plastics industry developed technologies to create a variety of plastics with broad applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlPlastics_Division_:_the_history_of_plastic_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlPlastics_Division_:_the_history_of_plastic-124">[124]</a></sup> Once <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> began, the US military used these new plastic materials to improve weapons, protect equipment, and to replace heavy components in aircraft and vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlPlastics_Division_:_the_history_of_plastic_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-urlPlastics_Division_:_the_history_of_plastic-124">[124]</a></sup> After WWII, manufacturers saw the potential plastics could have in many industries, and plastics were incorporated into new consumer product designs. Plastics began to replace wood and metal in existing products as well, and today plastics are the most widely used manufacturing materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlPlastics_Division_:_the_history_of_plastic_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-urlPlastics_Division_:_the_history_of_plastic-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the 1960s, all homes were wired with electricity and had numerous electrical appliances. Cotton had been the dominant textile used to produce home furnishings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> but now home furnishings were composed of mostly synthetic materials. More than 500 billion cigarettes were consumed each year in the 1960s, as compared to less than 3 billion per year in the beginning of the twentieth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.lungusa.org_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.lungusa.org-126">[126]</a></sup> When combined with high density living, the potential for home fires was higher in the 1960s than it had ever been in the US. By the late 1970s, approximately 6000 people in the US died each year in home fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.nfpa.org_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.nfpa.org-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1972, in response to this situation, the National Commission on Fire Prevention and Control was created to study the fire problem in the US. In 1973 they published their findings in America Burning, a 192-page report<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.usfa.dhs.gov_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.usfa.dhs.gov-128">[128]</a></sup> that made recommendations to increase fire prevention. Most of the recommendations dealt with fire prevention education and improved building engineering, such as the installation of fire sprinklers and smoke detectors. The Commission expected that with the recommendations, a 5% reduction in fire losses could be expected each year, halving the annual losses within 14 years.</p>
<p>Historically, treatments with alum and borax were used to reduce the flammability of fabric and wood, as far back as Roman times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHC192_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-EHC192-129">[129]</a></sup> Since it is a non-absorbent material once created, flame retardant chemicals are added to plastic during the polymerization reaction when it is formed. Organic compounds based on halogens like bromine and chlorine are used as the flame retardant additive in plastics, and in fabric based textiles as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHC192_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-EHC192-129">[129]</a></sup> The widespread use of brominated flame retardants may be due to the push from Great Lakes Chemical Corporation (GLCC) to profit from its huge investment in bromine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlGreat_Lakes_Chemical_Corporation_--_Company_History_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlGreat_Lakes_Chemical_Corporation_--_Company_History-130">[130]</a></sup> In 1992, the world market consumed approximately 150,000 tonnes of bromine-based flame retardants, and GLCC produced 30% of the world supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EHC192_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-EHC192-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>PBDEs have the potential to disrupt thyroid hormone balance and contribute to a variety of neurological and developmental deficits, including low intelligence and learning disabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlwww.epa.gov_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlwww.epa.gov-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-132">[132]</a></sup> Many of the most common PBDE's were banned in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18470294_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18470294-133">[133]</a></sup> Studies with rodents have suggested that even brief exposure to PBDEs can cause developmental and behavior problems in juvenile rodents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eriksson_2002_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eriksson_2002-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa_2007_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costa_2007-134">[134]</a></sup> and exposure interferes with proper thyroid hormone regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lema_2008_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lema_2008-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phthalates">Phthalates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Phthalates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phthalates" title="Phthalates">Phthalates</a></div>
<p>Phthalates are found in some soft toys, flooring, medical equipment, cosmetics and air fresheners. They are of potential health concern because they are known to disrupt the endocrine system of animals, and some research has implicated them in the rise of birth defects of the male reproductive system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swan_2005_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Swan_2005-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barrett2005_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett2005-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although an expert panel has concluded that there is "insufficient evidence" that they can harm the reproductive system of infants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16239449_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16239449-138">[138]</a></sup> California,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCalifornia_OKs_phthalates_ban_on_childrens_products_.7C_Health_.7C_Reuters_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCalifornia_OKs_phthalates_ban_on_childrens_products_.7C_Health_.7C_Reuters-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlChemical_.26_Engineering_News:_Latest_News_-_California_Bans_Phthalates_In_Toys_For_Children_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlChemical_.26_Engineering_News:_Latest_News_-_California_Bans_Phthalates_In_Toys_For_Children-140">[140]</a></sup><a class="external text" href="http://www.ecy.wa.gov/programs/hwtr/rtt/cspa/pdf/LeadCadmiumPhthalatesenforcement.pdf" rel="nofollow">Washington state</a> and Europe have banned them from toys. One phthalate, <a href="/wiki/Bis(2-ethylhexyl)_phthalate" title="Bis(2-ethylhexyl) phthalate">bis(2-ethylhexyl) phthalate</a> (DEHP), used in medical tubing, catheters and blood bags, may harm sexual development in male infants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher-136">[136]</a></sup> In 2002, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> released a public report which cautioned against exposing male babies to DEHP. Although there are no direct human studies the FDA report states: "Exposure to DEHP has produced a range of adverse effects in laboratory animals, but of greatest concern are effects on the development of the male reproductive system and production of normal sperm in young animals. In view of the available animal data, precautions should be taken to limit the exposure of the developing male to DEHP".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlUS_FDA.2FCDRH:_FDA_Public_Health_Notification:_PVC_Devices_Containing_the_Plasticizer_DEHP_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlUS_FDA.2FCDRH:_FDA_Public_Health_Notification:_PVC_Devices_Containing_the_Plasticizer_DEHP-141">[141]</a></sup> Similarly, phthalates may play a causal role in disrupting masculine neurological development when exposed prenatally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19919614_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19919614-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dibutyl_phthalate" title="Dibutyl phthalate">Dibutyl phthalate</a> (DBP) has also disrupted <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glucagon" title="Glucagon">glucagon</a> signaling in animal models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2016_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2016-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perfluorooctanoic_acid">Perfluorooctanoic acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Perfluorooctanoic acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PFOA" title="PFOA">PFOA</a> exerts hormonal effects including alteration of thyroid hormone levels. Blood serum levels of PFOA were associated with an increased time to pregnancy — or "infertility" — in a 2009 study. PFOA exposure is associated with decreased semen quality. PFOA appeared to act as an endocrine disruptor by a potential mechanism on breast maturation in young girls. A C8 Science Panel status report noted an association between exposure in girls and a later onset of puberty.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_suspected_endocrine_disruptors">Other suspected endocrine disruptors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other suspected endocrine disruptors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some other examples of putative EDCs are <a href="/wiki/Dioxins_and_dioxin-like_compounds" title="Dioxins and dioxin-like compounds">polychlorinated dibenzo-dioxins</a> (PCDDs) and <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_dibenzofurans" title="Polychlorinated dibenzofurans">-furans</a> (PCDFs), <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs), <a href="/wiki/Phenols" title="Phenols">phenol derivatives</a> and a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> (most prominent being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organochlorine" title="Organochlorine">organochlorine</a> insecticides like <a href="/wiki/Endosulfan" title="Endosulfan">endosulfan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kepone" title="Kepone">kepone</a> (chlordecone) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> and its derivatives, the herbicide <a href="/wiki/Atrazine" title="Atrazine">atrazine</a>, and the fungicide <a href="/wiki/Vinclozolin" title="Vinclozolin">vinclozolin</a>), the contraceptive 17-alpha <a href="/wiki/Ethinylestradiol" title="Ethinylestradiol">ethinylestradiol</a>, as well as naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Phytoestrogens" title="Phytoestrogens">phytoestrogens</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Genistein" title="Genistein">genistein</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycoestrogens" title="Mycoestrogens">mycoestrogens</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Zearalenone" title="Zearalenone">zearalenone</a>.</p>
<p>The molting in crustaceans is an endocrine-controlled process. In the marine penaeid shrimp <i><a href="/wiki/Whiteleg_shrimp" title="Whiteleg shrimp">Litopenaeus vannamei</a></i>, exposure to <a href="/wiki/Endosulfan" title="Endosulfan">endosulfan</a> resulted increased susceptibility to acute toxicity and increased mortalities in the postmolt stage of the shrimp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22119282_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22119282-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many sunscreens contain <a href="/wiki/Oxybenzone" title="Oxybenzone">oxybenzone</a>, a chemical blocker that provides broad-spectrum UV coverage, yet is subject to a lot of controversy due its potential estrogenic effect in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tributyltin" title="Tributyltin">Tributyltin</a> (TBT) are organotin compounds that for 40 years TBT was used as a biocide in anti-fouling paint, commonly known as bottom paint. TBT has been shown to impact invertebrate and vertebrate development, disrupting the endocrine system, resulting in masculinization, lower survival rates, as well as many health problems in mammals.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Temporal_trends_of_body_burden">Temporal trends of body burden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Temporal trends of body burden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since being banned, the average human body burdens of DDT and PCB have been declining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F.C3.BCrst_2006_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-F.C3.BCrst_2006-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knobeloch_2009_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knobeloch_2009-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nor.C3.A9n_2000_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nor.C3.A9n_2000-147">[147]</a></sup> Since their ban in 1972, the PCB body burden is 1/100 of what it was in the early 1980s (Weschler 2009). Monitoring programs of European breast milk samples have shown that PBDE levels are increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F.C3.BCrst_2006_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-F.C3.BCrst_2006-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nor.C3.A9n_2000_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nor.C3.A9n_2000-147">[147]</a></sup> An analysis of PBDE content in breast milk samples from Europe, Canada, and the US shows that levels are 40 times higher for North American women than for Swedish women, and that levels in North America are doubling every two to six years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hites_2004_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hites_2004-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betts_2002_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Betts_2002-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_approach">Legal approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Legal approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The multitude of possible endocrine disruptors are technically regulated in the United States by many laws, including: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic_Substances_Control_Act" title="Toxic Substances Control Act">Toxic Substances Control Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Insecticide,_Fungicide,_and_Rodenticide_Act" title="Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act">Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food,_Drug_and_Cosmetic_Act" title="Food, Drug and Cosmetic Act">Food, Drug and Cosmetic Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a>.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States">Congress of the United States</a> has improved the evaluation and regulation process of drugs and other chemicals. The <a href="/wiki/Food_Quality_Protection_Act" title="Food Quality Protection Act">Food Quality Protection Act</a> of 1996 and the <a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a> of 1996 simultaneously provided the first legislative direction requiring the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EPA" title="EPA">EPA</a> to address endocrine disruption through establishment of a program for screening and testing of chemical substances.</p>
<p>In 1998 the EPA announced the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_Disruptor_Screening_Program&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Endocrine Disruptor Screening Program (page does not exist)">Endocrine Disruptor Screening Program</a> by establishment of a framework for priority setting, screening and testing more than 85,000 chemicals in commerce. The basic concept behind the program is that prioritization will be based on existing information about chemical uses, production volume, structure-activity and toxicity. Screening is done by use of <i>in vitro</i> test systems (by examining, for instance, if an agent interacts with the <a href="/wiki/Estrogen_receptor" title="Estrogen receptor">estrogen receptor</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Androgen_receptor" title="Androgen receptor">androgen receptor</a>) and via the use of in animal models, such as development of tadpoles and uterine growth in prepubertal rodents. Full scale testing will examine effects not only in mammals (rats) but also in a number of other species (frogs, fish, birds and invertebrates). Since the theory involves the effects of these substances on a functioning system, animal testing is essential for scientific validity, but has been opposed by <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a> groups. Similarly, proof that these effects occur in humans would require human testing, and such testing also has opposition.</p>
<p>After failing to meet several deadlines to begin testing, the EPA finally announced that they were ready to begin the process of testing dozens of chemical entities that are suspected endocrine disruptors early in 2007, eleven years after the program was announced. When the final structure of the tests was announced there was objection to their design. Critics have charged that the entire process has been compromised by chemical company interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlScientists_criticize_EPA_chemical_screening_program_.7C_News_for_Dallas.2C_Texas_.7C_Dallas_Morning_News_.7C_Science_and_Medicine_.7C_Health_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlScientists_criticize_EPA_chemical_screening_program_.7C_News_for_Dallas.2C_Texas_.7C_Dallas_Morning_News_.7C_Science_and_Medicine_.7C_Health-150">[150]</a></sup> In 2005, the EPA appointed a panel of experts to conduct an open peer-review of the program and its orientation. Their results found that "the long-term goals and science questions in the EDC program are appropriate",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16882539_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16882539-151">[151]</a></sup> however this study was conducted over a year before the EPA announced the final structure of the screening program.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2013, a number of pesticides containing endocrine disrupting chemicals were in draft EU criteria to be banned. On the 2nd May, US <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_Trade_and_Investment_Partnership" title="Transatlantic Trade and Investment Partnership">TTIP</a> negotiators insisted the EU drop the criteria. They stated that a risk-based approach should be taken on regulation. Later the same day <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Day" title="Catherine Day">Catherine Day</a> wrote to Karl Falkenberg asking for the criteria to be removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian22may15_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian22may15-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> had been to set criteria by December 2013 identifying endocrine disrupting chemicals (EDCs) in thousands of products — including disinfectants, pesticides and toiletries — that have been linked to cancers, birth defects and development disorders in children. However, the body delayed the process, prompting Sweden to state that it would sue the commission in May 2014 — blaming chemical industry lobbying for the disruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-capitalfm.co.ke_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-capitalfm.co.ke-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>“This delay is due to the European chemical lobby, which put pressure again on different commissioners. Hormone disrupters are becoming a huge problem. In some places in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> we see double-sexed fish. We have scientific reports on how this affects fertility of young boys and girls, and other serious effects,” Swedish Environment Minister <a href="/wiki/Lena_Ek" title="Lena Ek">Lena Ek</a> told the <a href="/wiki/Agence_France-Presse" title="Agence France-Presse">AFP</a>, noting that <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> had also demanded action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-capitalfm.co.ke_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-capitalfm.co.ke-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nordic_Council_of_Ministers" title="Nordic Council of Ministers">Nordic Council of Ministers</a> released its own independent report that estimated the impact of environmental EDCs on male reproductive health, and the resulting cost to public health systems. It concluded that EDCs likely cost health systems across the EU anywhere from 59 million to 1.18 billion Euros a year, noting that even this represented only "a fraction of the endocrine related diseases".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_human_body_cleanup">Environmental and human body cleanup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Environmental and human body cleanup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is evidence that once a pollutant is no longer in use, or once its use is heavily restricted, the human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_burden" title="Body burden">body burden</a> of that pollutant declines. Through the efforts of several large-scale monitoring programs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCalifornia_Biomonitoring_Program.2C_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCalifornia_Biomonitoring_Program.2C-156">[156]</a></sup> the most prevalent pollutants in the human population are fairly well known. The first step in reducing the body burden of these pollutants is eliminating or phasing out their production.</p>
<p>The second step toward lowering human body burden is awareness of and potentially labeling foods that are likely to contain high amounts of pollutants. This strategy has worked in the past - pregnant and nursing women are cautioned against eating seafood that is known to accumulate high levels of mercury. Ideally,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2011)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> a certification process should be in place to routinely test animal products for POP concentrations. This would help the consumer identify which foods have the highest levels of pollutants.</p>
<p>The most challenging aspect<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of this problem is discovering how to eliminate these compounds from the environment and where to focus remediation efforts. Even pollutants no longer in production persist in the environment, and bio-accumulate in the food chain. An understanding of how these chemicals, once in the environment, move through ecosystems, is essential to designing ways to isolate and remove them. Working backwards through the food chain may help to identify areas to prioritize for remediation efforts. This may be extremely challenging for contaminated fish and marine mammals that have a large habitat and who consume fish from many different areas throughout their lives.</p>
<p>Many persistent organic compounds, PCB, DDT and PBDE included, accumulate in river and marine sediments. Several processes are currently being used by the EPA to clean up heavily polluted areas, as outlined in their Green Remediation program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Overview_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Overview-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most interesting ways is the utilization of naturally occurring microbes that degrade PCB congeners to remediate contaminated areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Field_2008_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Field_2008-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are many success stories of cleanup efforts of large heavily contaminated Superfund sites. A 10-acre (40,000 m<sup>2</sup>) landfill in Austin, Texas contaminated with illegally dumped <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">VOCs</a> was restored in a year to a wetland and educational park.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Profiles_of_Green_Strategies_.7C_Rhizome_Collective_Inc._Brownfield_Site.2C_Austin.2C_TX_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Profiles_of_Green_Strategies_.7C_Rhizome_Collective_Inc._Brownfield_Site.2C_Austin.2C_TX-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>A US uranium enrichment site that was contaminated with uranium and PCBs was cleaned up with high tech equipment used to find the pollutants within the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Profiles_.26_Case_Studies_of_Green_Remediation_.7C_Paducah_Gaseous_Diffusion_Plant.2C_Paducah.2C_KY_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Profiles_.26_Case_Studies_of_Green_Remediation_.7C_Paducah_Gaseous_Diffusion_Plant.2C_Paducah.2C_KY-160">[160]</a></sup> The soil and water at a polluted wetlands site were cleaned of VOCs, PCBs and lead, native plants were installed as biological filters, and a community program was implemented to ensure ongoing monitoring of pollutant concentrations in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Profiles_.26_Case_Studies_of_Green_Remediation:_Re-Solve.2C_Inc..2C_North_Dartmouth.2C_MA_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlCLU-IN.ORG_.7C_Green_Remediation_.7C_Profiles_.26_Case_Studies_of_Green_Remediation:_Re-Solve.2C_Inc..2C_North_Dartmouth.2C_MA-161">[161]</a></sup> These case studies are encouraging due to the short amount of time needed to remediate the site and the high level of success achieved.</p>
<p>Studies suggest that bisphenol A,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22253637_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22253637-162">[162]</a></sup> certain PCBs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24083032_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24083032-163">[163]</a></sup> and phthalate compounds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23213291_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23213291-164">[164]</a></sup> are preferentially eliminated from the human body through sweat.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Economic effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Human exposure may cause some health effects, such as lower IQ and adult obesity. These effects may lead to lost productivity, disability, or premature death in some people. One source estimated that, within the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, this economic effect might have about twice the <a href="/wiki/Economic_impact_analysis" title="Economic impact analysis">economic impact</a> as the effects caused by mercury and lead contamination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>The socio-economic burden of endocrine disrupting chemicals (EDC)-associated health effects for the European Union was estimated based on currently available literature and considering the uncertainties with respect to causality with EDCs and corresponding health-related costs to be in the range of €46 billion to €288 billion per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Endocrine_disruptor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 33em; -webkit-column-width: 33em; column-width: 33em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
